  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

        state: 'apple_oauth_state_5b4c5c5b-a9fc-4055-84a0-7737237a72d1'

<span id="freeTextContainer17587640907103077616">Janey lived in the locked room. Twice a day the Persian slave trader came in and taught her to be a whore. Otherwise there was nothing.   <span id="freeText17587640907103077616" style="display:none">Janey lived in the locked room. Twice a day the Persian slave trader came in and taught her to be a whore. Otherwise there was nothing. One day she found a pencil stub and scrap of paper in a forgotten corner of the room. She began to write down her life, starting with "Parents stink" (her father, who is also her boyfriend, has fallen in love with another woman and is about to leave her). <br /><br />With <i>Blood and Guts in High School</i>, Kathy Acker, whose work has been labeled everything from post-punk porn to post-punk feminism, has created a brilliantly subversive narrative built from conversation, description, conjecture, and moments snatched from history and literature.<br /><br />~ groveatlantic.com</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/237647964?book_show_action=true">Nov 20, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Shovelmonkey1" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Shovelmonkey1" href="/user/show/3926982-shovelmonkey1">Shovelmonkey1</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer237647964" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5653303186702281305">Again I turn to the creators and compilers of the 1001 books to read before you die list and ask, "Why?"<br/>Yes, noted scholars, authors and critics who form the all seeing literary eye that is the 1001 books list, "Why do you hate people who read books? And why do you want to punish them so?"<br/><br/>I know the list is supposed to represent many novels, genres and styles not for their likeability but for their uniqueness and their gift to literature and the world at large, but come on. Blood and Guts in Hig</span>
  <span id="freeText5653303186702281305" style="display:none">Again I turn to the creators and compilers of the 1001 books to read before you die list and ask, "Why?"<br />Yes, noted scholars, authors and critics who form the all seeing literary eye that is the 1001 books list, "Why do you hate people who read books? And why do you want to punish them so?"<br /><br />I know the list is supposed to represent many novels, genres and styles not for their likeability but for their uniqueness and their gift to literature and the world at large, but come on. Blood and Guts in High School? Really? Yougottabekiddinme!<br /><br />Rarely has my eye brow spent so much time raised over one piece of prose. Eventually the right eye brow got tired of being sceptically raised and I had to give it a rest and raise the left one instead. I'm giving this book one star because I can't give it half a star and because if I gave it no stars then that would imply a state of unratedness which might lead people to erroneously conclude at first glance that I loved it so much I felt there were not enough stars available or that I was too lazy or forgetful to apply any stars at all. <br /><br />To my mind this was an exercise in juvenile and moderately pointless self indulgence with a little bit of crap porn thrown in. The ground breaking theatrically scrap book style smells of laziness not genius to me and it is hard to discern the smell of either of things beneath the even headier smell of mendacity because surely this was intended as nothing more than a practical joke? This book is the Emperors new clothes. Someone said it was genius and others followed suit (or suitless as the case may be). </span>
  <a data-text-id="5653303186702281305" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2572384056" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2572384056" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32804270-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1564311252p2/32804270.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2572384056?book_show_action=true">Mar 14, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/32804270-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32804270-michael?shelf=2019">2019</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2572384056" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7285394770034348977">Sensational and cynical, <i>Blood and Guts in High School</i> aspires to unsettle. In fast-moving, terse prose, full of lurid images, Acker dramatizes the many trials faced by the novel’s protagonist Janey after her boyfriend/father abandons her for another woman. The author rebels against linear narrative and coherent characterization at every turn, whether she’s describing her heroine’s imprisonment by a Persian slave trader or following her tumultuous romance with a cartoonish version of Jean Genet.</span>
  <span id="freeText7285394770034348977" style="display:none">Sensational and cynical, <i>Blood and Guts in High School</i> aspires to unsettle. In fast-moving, terse prose, full of lurid images, Acker dramatizes the many trials faced by the novel’s protagonist Janey after her boyfriend/father abandons her for another woman. The author rebels against linear narrative and coherent characterization at every turn, whether she’s describing her heroine’s imprisonment by a Persian slave trader or following her tumultuous romance with a cartoonish version of Jean Genet. Forcing readers to experience rather than understand her work, Acker darts between third and first person, prose and poetry, text and illustration; she inserts a modernized rewrite of <i>The Scarlet Letter</i> near the book’s end, as well as a series of grotesque fairytales near its start; she juxtaposes trashy pornographic scenes with cryptic treatises on anarchism. While this kind of surreal anti-art might have been shocking in the eighties, it’s lost much of its edge, and the book feels a bit bland today.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7285394770034348977" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText8992392653346183306" style="display:none">You know those two assholes who break up at every party? By the end of the party they’ll be slobbering all over each other again and eventually they’ll fuck on your parents bed and leave some sort of gross stain that you’ll never be able to explain even to yourself.<br><br>So this is a deep dive into these idiots and their shitty relationship, and the book is exactly like they are: fun for like two minutes in sortof a “Lol, get a load of these knuckleheads” way and then very, eternally tiresome. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1564276073i/27897354._SX540_.jpg" alt="acker" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><i>This is Kathy Acker, whose back is cooler than her book</i><br><br>Also Janey is maybe really Johnny’s ten-year-old daughter, so there’s that. <span id="freeTextContainer286618363983943079">If you’re going to make a statement, best jump in at the deep end straight away. And so, the first ten pages are a bevy of delights, a veritable cornucopia of plenty, a smorgasbord of every vile, disgusting, grotesque act known to man. Ten year old Janey is being buttfucked by daddy, because her cunt is red raw from VD, probably acquired from when daddy’s best friend Billy had a go. In between blowing both of them off. The bonus track is a stache of lithographs of daddy’s pecker, in all manners </span>
  <span id="freeText286618363983943079" style="display:none">If you’re going to make a statement, best jump in at the deep end straight away. And so, the first ten pages are a bevy of delights, a veritable cornucopia of plenty, a smorgasbord of every vile, disgusting, grotesque act known to man. Ten year old Janey is being buttfucked by daddy, because her cunt is red raw from VD, probably acquired from when daddy’s best friend Billy had a go. In between blowing both of them off. The bonus track is a stache of lithographs of daddy’s pecker, in all manners of repose, and not: occasionally there are squirts of gism for special effects. And if I’m not staring at the great big eye of daddy’s winking willy, theres plenty of female genitalia in this gallery opening to keep me busy.<br /><br />Janet is quickly abandoned and sent to board in NYC, where she whores it a bit (well a lot), has an impressive number of abortions and eventually ends up as a sex slave to an eighty year old Iranian (as you do). The finale sees her (now at the grand old age of 14) in Tangiers, lording it about with Jean Genet (the novel’s piece d’ resistance). And then she dies. The end.<br /><br />And it never happened, so thats OK then. <br /><br />Poems, epistolary motifs, confessional modes, book reviews, some clever stuff with pagination and text breaks, and loads of scribbly pictures flesh out the storyboard.<br /><br />Whilst I appreciate the non too subtle message of female sexualisation, exploitation and degradation, the curve balls keep coming hard and fast, but always from left field. So, mono-dimensional diatribe, perhaps seeking to numb with repetitive mantra even though the palette of media truncates. <br /><br />Hard, fast, frenetic and unapologetic, the imagery leaves a cold, disquieting impression and a desperate want for a shower, or something. Cleansing.<br /><br /></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2532586864" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2532586864" />
    <a title="Fede" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/73964263-fede"><img alt="Fede" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1604333859p2/73964263.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2532586864?book_show_action=true">Sep 26, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fede" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fede" href="/user/show/73964263-fede">Fede</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/36133479-blood-and-guts-in-high-school">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/73964263-fede?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/73964263-fede?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/73964263-fede?shelf=american-canadian">american-canadian</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2532586864" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3686986275219250005">I knew I would love this one. It was written in the stars and I could have shelved it as a fave without even reading it. <br/><br/>'Reading' is an inadequate word though: in fact I am utterly unable to think of it as a mere reading experience. Acker's book is indeed a challenge to any attempt to define what a novel is supposed to be, a challenge that sucked me in, deeper and deeper and then higher and higher, from the very first page.<br/>Text, illustration, mixed fonts, pages written in the author's weird han</span>
  <span id="freeText3686986275219250005" style="display:none">I knew I would love this one. It was written in the stars and I could have shelved it as a fave without even reading it. <br /><br />'Reading' is an inadequate word though: in fact I am utterly unable to think of it as a mere reading experience. Acker's book is indeed a challenge to any attempt to define what a novel is supposed to be, a challenge that sucked me in, deeper and deeper and then higher and higher, from the very first page.<br />Text, illustration, mixed fonts, pages written in the author's weird handwriting, narrative paragraphs that become diary excepts turning into poems which merge into drawings, maps, graffiti, translations of latin odes and blatant, parodic plagiarism: this book is a postmodern Wunderkammer and I got lost in it as soon as I took a glimpse of its decadent monstrosity. <br />Thank God I didn't read this as a teenager.<br /><br />Now, it's easy to look at "Blood and Guts" as literary filth, sold as a masterpiece of experimental fiction. It's quite comfortable to call it garbage and pretend not to see what it actually is; its outrageous contents are better dealt with if we deny the meaning and depth of the outrage. <br />The truth is that Acker's work must be analysed from two different perspectives: exoterically and esoterically. <br />That's exactly what I'll try to do. <br /><br />1)The exoteric concept:<br /><br />The book opens with a dialogue that seems to be taken from a French Nouvelle Vague film: a ten (!) year old child named Janey is dumped by her father, who is also her fiancé (yep).The guy can't stand the pressure of a long-term relationship, he needs his space and Janey is preventing him from being himself. By the way, she also suffers from pelvic inflammatory desease, guess why. Among the Mayan ruins of Merida, a disquiting landscape of unintelligible architecture and nature, they go through silences, sex, jealousy, sex, nostalgic memories, sex, incommunicabilty. They both know the romance is over. <br />Janey is therefore sent to New York and, until she turns fourteen, she goes through a daily routine of drugs, junior high school, street gangs, syphilis, abortions, underpaid jobs, filthy dwellings in the city slums. To make things even worse, one day a Persian dwarf and slave trader (don't forget this is a Kathy Acker <br />book) has her kidnapped and locked in a room, where she's thoroughly trained in whoring techniques in order to walk the streets for the dwarf. <br />You think it's enough? Nope. Unfortunately, Janey has cancer. When she realises that her beloved Slave Trader is going to leave her, she escapes to Tangier, the Moroccan haven for Beatniks, artists and junkies, where she meets Jean Genet. The strange couple sets off on a hallucinatory journey to Egypt in search of an ancient book that teaches how men can change their nature and become... well, something else. Doves, alligators, souls, who knows. Whatever it is, it's better than what we are now. <br />So they find the book, have a glimpse of its contents and Janey dies. <br />The end. <br /><br />Okay. <br />Now, if you're still reading, please let me point out a few more things that seem to be conveniently overlooked by most 'serious' reviewers (though I wonder whether they've actually read the novel before trashing it). <br /><br />2) The esoterical revelation:<br /><br />This is a postmodern coming-of-age tale. A disgusting, painful, terribly serious tale in which the protagonist undergoes any sort of mental and physical alienation.<br />Alienation, not abuse: there's no abuse here. The violence has been completely metabolised by the child's mind, to the point that it's not even perceived as such anymore.<br />When Janey is abandoned by her father/lover, she finally realises how desperate she is for love. In the big city she's swallowed by a downward spiral of loneliness, lack of feeling, lack of affection, and starts looking for a compensation in raw sex and sheer violence: any man is a means for her to feel protected from her own needs. <br />At this point the author makes an amazingly clever analysis of her character's psychology. The effect is just hilarious: a little girl in her early teens talking like a Jungian shrink or a French existentialist. Furthermore, Acker makes a merciless satire of modern society: the episode of Janey's first abortion is unforgettable, with a chilling description of the assembly-line work of the doctors and nurses performing it. <br /><br />The world in which Janey lives is a jail run by a cannibalistic society that feeds on the kids' need for love and understanding. The Mayan temples surrounding her house in Mexico remind us indeed of the ritual slaughtering of kids as offerings and scapegoats; the ancient tribes killed their offspring in order to appease their gods, whereas our society sacrifices kids to its lust and dissatisfaction. <br />This is not a story of pedophilia and child abuse, for the very simple reason that there is no child here. Janey is treated, beaten, fucked, talked to like an adult, or rather a slave who has neither age nor identity. We hardly remember the protagonist is a ten-year-old child as we read the dialogue between Janey and her 'adulterous' father, written in the form of a script or play (pure dialogue, in which emotions are left aside). <br /><br />The Persian dwarf and Slave Trader (a crossing between Twin Peaks and Pierre Guyotat) is the grotesque spokesman of the author's viewpoint with regard to culture and art, seen as the only way out for mankind. In his own words:<br />"Culture is our highest form of life. And it is literature more than any other art which enables us to grasp this higher life, for literature is the most abstract of the arts. It is the only art which is not sensual." <br />Language as a weapon and a shield, the only means to forward any true act of rebellion; otherwise, the lack of a proper language makes freedom suicidal and pointless. <br />Hence Janey's urge to tell us about her life: through words, obscene drawings (don't read this in public if you still care about respectability), imaginary maps we must decipher like Egyptian hieroglyphics... as long as we find a way to express ourselves, says Acker, we aren't the defeated, broken creatures 'they' want us to be: we are still in control of our minds, bodies, feelings, despite the state of slavery in which we are meant to live. Because self-expression means judgement, either it is to condemn or to absolve: <br />"Either I judge and blame and Hell exists, or I don't judge and everything's OK. Either this is a time for total despair or it's a time of madness." <br /><br />In one of the most complex parts of the book, Janey identifies herself with Hester Prynne, the protagonist of Hawthorne's "The Scarlet Letter": a victim of the old puritanism morphs into a victim of our cold, nihilist world. <br />Acker makes a good point here: <br />"Hawthorne had to protect himself so he could keep writing. Right now I can speak as directly as I want 'cause no one gives a shit about writing and ideas, all amyone cares about is money." <br />She knew her book wouldn't be banned, censored, burnt on the stake... quite the contrary. It would sell pretty well and its devastating power would be tamed by commercial success and intellectual indifference. <br />What a shame. I wonder whether the Holy Office wasn't more sensitive to culture than we are nowadays. It certainly recognised the revolutionary charge of books, and knew where their potential can lead. Alas, we've become too open-minded to be really outraged by anything; after all, Janey's is the world we've wanted - and created. <br /><br />"We are dreaming of sex,<br />of thieves, murderers, <br />firebrands,<br />of huge thighs opening <br />to us like this night.<br />So we create this world<br />in our own image."<br /><br />The final part of the novel is entirely graphic: Janey and her mentor Genet (whose homosexuality prevents him from loving the girl: yet another delusion and failure) find the mysterious book and learn how to go 'beyond' their human nature. We don't know what the secret is, though it does work, for Janey's death is kind of a catharsis. The shortest ever, as far as I know: "She dies", says Acker. That's all. <br /><br />Janey is the symbol of innocence in the modern world, and her story is an allegory depicting how it is stifled and exploited. It's also a heartrending, often poetic celebration of its potential: "We are all alike, we are all immaculately crazy".<br />Despite the filth, the obscenity, the nihilism, this is basically a tale of hope. It's a journey through hell that leads nonetheless to some indefinite redemption. <br /><br />" Soon many other Janeys were born and these Janeys covered the world."</span>
  <a data-text-id="3686986275219250005" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_866618296" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/866618296" />
    <a title="Mala" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5960716-mala"><img alt="Mala" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1435586901p2/5960716.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/866618296?book_show_action=true">Feb 27, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mala" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mala" href="/user/show/5960716-mala">Mala</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5960716-mala?shelf=disturbing">disturbing</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5960716-mala?shelf=metafiction">metafiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5960716-mala?shelf=controversial">controversial</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5960716-mala?shelf=female-writer">female-writer</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer866618296" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10239974802198254176">It is so hard to like this book with its clunky prose, weird drawings, garbled thinking, bizarre narrative, a sex-obsessed protagonist...<br/>But once in a while, after such horror-laden passages, you come across something like this that brings a lump to your throat:<br/>"Most people are what they sense and if all you see day after day is a mat on a floor that belongs to the rats and four walls with tiny piles of plaster at the bottom, and all you eat is starch, and all you hear is continuous noise, you s</span>
  <span id="freeText10239974802198254176" style="display:none">It is so hard to like this book with its clunky prose, weird drawings, garbled thinking, bizarre narrative, a sex-obsessed protagonist...<br />But once in a while, after such horror-laden passages, you come across something like this that brings a lump to your throat:<br />"Most people are what they sense and if all you see day after day is a mat on a floor that belongs to the rats and four walls with tiny piles of plaster at the bottom, and all you eat is starch, and all you hear is continuous noise, you smell garbage and piss which drips through the walls continually, and all the people you know live like you, it's not horrible, it's just . . .Who they are."<br /><br />Kathy's self-deprecatory humour is the saving grace here which while laughing out loud also moistens your eyes- esp. mention here of the madcap "Inside a small East Village bakery" scene: "what's that cooky?"<br />And that hilarious feminist deconstruction of <i>The Scarlet Letter</i>, the Latinate versification – yeah, it's a mash up of many genres &amp; styles.<br />But those diamonds in the dross are so few &amp; far between that it mainly becomes an exercise in tedium - I just couldn't wait for this book to be over &amp; done with.<br /><br />You'll say "Oh but what about that magic word-Experimental?"<br />More like 'Mental' I shd say!<br /><br />Susan E. Hawkins has analysed this book as a postmodernist work in her article "All in the Family: Kathy Acker’s Blood and Guts in High School.” Contemporary Literature 45.4 (2004).<br />From Wikipedia:<br />"According to Hawkins, Acker is motivated by two discourses: the oedipal and the imperial (642). Using the mechanism of sexual and economic oppression, Acker is able to actualize the taboo surrounding incest by associating it with capitalism to demystify the oedipal formation of desire in the Western culture (Hawkins 646)."<br /><br />That's true but the fact remains that even when Janey is out of the influence of her father, she remains addicted to sex &amp; hardly makes much of an effort to change her life. Also the fact that Acker's female protagonist is a mere ten year old who dies by the age of fourteen doesn't really give her much of a say in how her life is run.<br />It was really frustrating reading these lines:<br />"...despite her high fever, she walked the streets. Where could she run to? Where was peace (someone who loved her)? No one would take her in. It was raining lightly. The rain was going to increase her infection. She stood in front of Sally's house. Then she made herself walk away. She walked back into her father's and her apartment. She hated the apartment. She didn't know what to do with her hateful tormented mind."<br /><br />Unlike the others, I wasn't appalled by the 'incest' angle because it is clear Janey doesn't have the priviledge of an adult perspective / value judgement on her life: this is life exactly how she has known it &amp; she relays it as such.<br /><br />Also that weird thing abt President Carter– how could Acker get away with such nonsense here when Coover had to face so many problems with <i>The Public Burning</i>?<br /><br />The edition I read was Blood and Guts in High School, plus two: ( Great Expectations; My Death My Life by Pier Paolo Pasolini [1984]) – and I was driven so brain-dead by this book that I didn't even notice there were two more books in it! And went on with glazed eyes in my dogged reading ( almost 420 pages of it, a parody of Macbeth kind of woke me up &amp; it was followed by a spoof on The Merchant of Venice, damn!), but I'm not really to be blamed as these also continue more or less the same themes, the same techniques – a female in dysfunctional surroundings, exploited by one &amp; all, obsessed with sex, raging against capitalism, religion, social structures - no offense to her fans, but maybe if you've read one of her books; you've read them all!<br /><br />Some readers might enjoy Acker's pastiche-driven, typographically varied narrative but that didn't work for this reader. There is a reason why folks on Gr have shelf named dropped / abandoned. Time I got one of those.<br /><br />Gift it to a frenemy- the good &amp; the bad parts of this book will nicely balance the good &amp; bad part of that friendship.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10239974802198254176" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/329813056" />
    <a title="MJ Nicholls" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2386804-mj-nicholls"><img alt="MJ Nicholls" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1374341850p2/2386804.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/329813056?book_show_action=true">May 14, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="MJ Nicholls" class="user" itemprop="url" name="MJ Nicholls" href="/user/show/2386804-mj-nicholls">MJ Nicholls</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=poems">poems</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=merkins">merkins</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=distaff">distaff</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=novels">novels</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer329813056" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer144424382828097786">This book was released in 1978, making “post-punk feminism” an apposite description. Not that punk had stopped in 1978 but post-punk bands were already evolving the two-chord assault of the punk sound into something better (or in the case of Howard Devoto or Jonathan Richman <i>before</i> punk even happened). So this hysterical novel chimes perfectly with the nascent post-punk art explosion coming from NY in the late seventies, entwining it with this glorious period of art-school pretention and irredee</span>
  <span id="freeText144424382828097786" style="display:none">This book was released in 1978, making “post-punk feminism” an apposite description. Not that punk had stopped in 1978 but post-punk bands were already evolving the two-chord assault of the punk sound into something better (or in the case of Howard Devoto or Jonathan Richman <i>before</i> punk even happened). So this hysterical novel chimes perfectly with the nascent post-punk art explosion coming from NY in the late seventies, entwining it with this glorious period of art-school pretention and irredeemable poetry. The novel uses typographical attack weapons (enormous fonts! childish scribblings! decorative borders!) to tell its horrible story of an abused feminine receptacle desperate to free herself from the whoredom of men. Along the way, she meets Genet who also proves himself to be a prick. Tsk. Something of a response to William H. Gass’s potentially sexist typographical funhouse, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/156189.Willie_Masters__Lonesome_Wife__American_Literature__Dalkey_Archive__" title="Willie Masters' Lonesome Wife (American Literature (Dalkey Archive)) by William H. Gass" rel="nofollow noopener">Willie Masters' Lonesome Wife</a>, perhaps? A perverse relic: mainly dark and funny now, too hysterical to make any longstanding feminist impact. I hate the cover. I feel I’m supposed to.</span>
  <a data-text-id="144424382828097786" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_347121573" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/347121573" />
    <a title="Nate D" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/406701-nate-d"><img alt="Nate D" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1203897989p2/406701.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/347121573?book_show_action=true">Jun 12, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nate D" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nate D" href="/user/show/406701-nate-d">Nate D</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">street kids, who will kind it beneath debris in the last squat in the LES.</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Nate D by: </span>
      journal found in nested boxes beneath the east river
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=70s-delerium">70s-delerium</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=post-modernism">post-modernism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=read-in-2012">read-in-2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=no-wave-new-york">no-wave-new-york</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer347121573" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3672267695790666285"><blockquote>Blood and guts in high school<br/>This is all I know<br/>Parents teachers boyfriends<br/>All have got to go<br/><br/>Some folks like trains,<br/> some folks like ships,<br/>I like the way you move you hips<br/>All I want is a taste of your lips,<br/> boy,<br/>All I want is a taste of your lips.</blockquote><br/>I'm glad I worked up to this via Acker's first two published novels, which were messy and serially enjoyable, because thye underscore all her techniques while also revealing how elegantly arranged and plotted this one is. Insane and ragged as it is (must </span>
  <span id="freeText3672267695790666285" style="display:none"><blockquote>Blood and guts in high school<br />This is all I know<br />Parents teachers boyfriends<br />All have got to go<br /><br />Some folks like trains,<br /> some folks like ships,<br />I like the way you move you hips<br />All I want is a taste of your lips,<br /> boy,<br />All I want is a taste of your lips.</blockquote><br />I'm glad I worked up to this via Acker's first two published novels, which were messy and serially enjoyable, because thye underscore all her techniques while also revealing how elegantly arranged and plotted this one is. Insane and ragged as it is (must be), she's cutting up and repurposing lit canon and her own life with a deft touch here, horrifying and hilarious and relentlessly unpredictable, interspersing any shock moments with crystal-eyed insight and poetic beauty (though rarely in the actual Poetry bits in here, which serve other purposes).<br /><blockquote>I walked along a highway. I was looking for a place to sit down, for some grass I could walk in, for a wood I could explore. I walked for hours. All the land on both sides of the highway, cultivated and wild, was private. I had to keep walking on the highway. I thought that people today, when they move move only by car, train, boat, or plane and so move only on roads. They perceive only the roads, the map, the prison. I think it's becoming harder to get off the roads.</blockquote><br />Of course, there's this ebbing-and-flowing level of total high school journal mawkishness and emotionallity and raging vulnerability, but that's the point. Your rage at age 14 was not misplaced and your should never let those flames get sanded over because they have never never stopped being real. It's just useful, easier to socialize and forget, isn't it? I'm also reminded of an old zine I found in a exhibition of sorts: Kathleen Hanna (of Bikini Kill) writing about her crush on Evan Dando, circa the early 90s. The same kind of self-aware perceptive juvenalia, the same invitation. I wasn't surprised to find out that meeting Kathy Acker was some kind of significant event for Hanna.<br /><br />I won't claim to catch more than a fraction of the references here, Acker being better-read by age 30 than I, it seems, but that's just fine, this offers plenty regardless, and I'll be happy to keep this in the back of my mind as I discover the sources for years to come.<br /><br /><blockquote>Teachers teach you intricate ways of saying one thing and doing something else.</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="3672267695790666285" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anthony Vacca" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anthony Vacca" href="/user/show/27183668-anthony-vacca">Anthony Vacca</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1147867716" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17030180255339881975">Poor pliant Janey just wants to be loved, but when her father dumps her at the age of 10 for a sexier girlfriend, and then ships her off to a New York boarding school, her only option, naturally, is to fall in with a street gang called the Scorpions and devote her pre-teens to a life of petty theft, random sex and multiple abortions. By the time Janey is 14, she has been kidnapped by a malaprop-cracking dwarf and forced into a life of prostitution. She passes the days writing her slaver love poe</span>
  <span id="freeText17030180255339881975" style="display:none">Poor pliant Janey just wants to be loved, but when her father dumps her at the age of 10 for a sexier girlfriend, and then ships her off to a New York boarding school, her only option, naturally, is to fall in with a street gang called the Scorpions and devote her pre-teens to a life of petty theft, random sex and multiple abortions. By the time Janey is 14, she has been kidnapped by a malaprop-cracking dwarf and forced into a life of prostitution. She passes the days writing her slaver love poems in Persian, until he also dumps her. And away Janey goes to Tangier, to hook up with the French writer Genet while utilizing her sense of self-loathing to stir up a revolution in Egypt against the powers that be (misogynistic bureaucrats with names like Mr. Blowjob).<br /><br />But all this summarizing is misleading; <i>Blood and Guts in High School</i> operates mainly as a trampoline to bounce off different experimental techniques (in heavy debt to William S. Burroughs) that further Acker's feminist agenda against 2000 years of men dominating women, mind, body and <i>prose</i>. Crude poetry written in scatter-shot free verse and rude drawings of his and hers genitalia pack a lot of the pages of this over-sized but slim novel. Most of the other pages carry screenplay-formatted conversations or cartoonish depictions of pedophilia. But Acker's lack of restraint, narrative focus and characterization are made up for with the energy, typographical chicanery and ferocity with which she rails against the prison bars of gender conformity. I approve. Especially the snide, punk-rock book report on the <i>The Scarlet Letter</i>. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17030180255339881975" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer9761330" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10092708045072418853">okay a disclaimer. this book is weird and racist and has fucked up incest fantasies and a million things that might make your skin crawl. it's also poetic and experimental in a way that was really inspiring for me as a nineteen year old trying to find my voice. like, none of the rules about writing matter, you can create your own punctuation and grammar, you can change the spelling of your main character's name halfway through, you can explore sexuality in the way it really feels which is not a </span>
  <span id="freeText10092708045072418853" style="display:none">okay a disclaimer. this book is weird and racist and has fucked up incest fantasies and a million things that might make your skin crawl. it's also poetic and experimental in a way that was really inspiring for me as a nineteen year old trying to find my voice. like, none of the rules about writing matter, you can create your own punctuation and grammar, you can change the spelling of your main character's name halfway through, you can explore sexuality in the way it really feels which is not a straight line more like a jagged forest of follicles and razor blades. it is the most readable kathy acker book and the only one that i liked (i think i read about five, there are more). i read this around the time that the movie "natural born killers" came out and i think together they pulled up all my shit that i didn't know how to name. they let me revel in the horror of survivors' revenge and glory stories. this book is not a nice healing adventure. it's scary and kind of awesome in that way. as far as the racist stuff i kind of noted it and put it aside while i was reading, i think maybe she's trying to address racism but it feels like she's perpetuating it. but i personally don't know if i've figured out as a white person how to address racism in my own fiction much better, i'm working on it...</span>

<div id="review_281479903" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/281479903" />
    <a title="Nathan &quot;N.R.&quot; Gaddis" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7607437-nathan-n-r-gaddis"><img alt="Nathan &quot;N.R.&quot; Gaddis" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1548295774p2/7607437.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/281479903?book_show_action=true">Apr 23, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nathan &quot;N.R.&quot; Gaddis" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nathan &quot;N.R.&quot; Gaddis" href="/user/show/7607437-nathan-n-r-gaddis">Nathan &quot;N.R.&quot; Gaddis</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7607437-nathan-n-r-gaddis?shelf=cnp-review">cnp-review</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7607437-nathan-n-r-gaddis?shelf=2017-gelesen">2017-gelesen</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer281479903" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4929529395099251503"><b>
  <i>
    <u>"Compendiously Nipping Pastiche. Clearseeing."</u>
  </i>
</b><br/><br/><br/>-cunt<br/>-fuck<br/><br/>...but for me this was evidence that some people believe feminism trumps good writing.<br/><br/>This book is the Emperors new clothes. [sic!]<br/><br/> sucky post-modernist writing. <br/><br/>But it's really perverted and I felt a little violated reading it. Incest, rape, domestic violence, etc. etc. <br/><br/>well, i'm going to give it to you anyway. <br/><br/>We've been punked, folks.<br/><br/>This was one of the most off-putting and obnoxious pieces of literature I've faced recently.<br/><br/>S</span>
  <span id="freeText4929529395099251503" style="display:none"><b>
  <i>
    <u>"Compendiously Nipping Pastiche. Clearseeing."</u>
  </i>
</b><br /><br /><br />-cunt<br />-fuck<br /><br />...but for me this was evidence that some people believe feminism trumps good writing.<br /><br />This book is the Emperors new clothes. [sic!]<br /><br /> sucky post-modernist writing. <br /><br />But it's really perverted and I felt a little violated reading it. Incest, rape, domestic violence, etc. etc. <br /><br />well, i'm going to give it to you anyway. <br /><br />We've been punked, folks.<br /><br />This was one of the most off-putting and obnoxious pieces of literature I've faced recently.<br /><br />She's just a parasite. Then again, that's pretty harsh. <br /><br />Yes, noted scholars, authors and critics who form the all seeing literary eye that is the 1001 books list, "Why do you hate people who read books? And why do you want to punish them so?"<br /><br />...this trash via the "1001" list are laughing their collective asses off...pathetic prank.<br /><br />Yougottabekiddinme!<br /><br />...this was thoroughly unenjoyable, and completely unenlightening....<br /><br />Absolutely the worst book I have ever read, and I've read a lot of books.<br /><br />basically, it's horrific.<br /><br />...an exercise in juvenile and moderately pointless self indulgence with a little bit of crap porn thrown in. <br /><br />...I wanted to gouge my eyes out.<br /><br />Seriously, what's this supposed to be? I love a good anti-novel as much as the next pretentious hipster, but don't just slap together a bunch of stuff and then bore me to death with it.<br /><br />But I just really despised the author's style. <br /><br />...smells of laziness not genius ... beneath the even headier smell of mendacity because ... nothing more than a practical joke?<br /><br />Did the author read A Clockwork Orange and Wild Animus, do some really strong pharmaceuticals and then decide she could write a book more disjointed and senseless than those two books ?<br /><br />...because it's on the 1001 Books You Must Read Before You Die list.<br /><br />I'd rather have read the ingredient list on my shampoo bottle than to have wasted my time on this tripe.<br /><br />It wasn't the fact that the main character in this novel was sleeping with her father. <br /><br />...this has absolutely no literary value. <br /><br />This is undeniably the worst piece of crap I've ever read.<br /><br />I'd give this book zero stars, but I'm compelled to rate it both as a warning to my fellow readers, and to do my small part to help bring down its overinflated rating. [noble!]<br /><br />...to me like an outburst of loathing; <br /><br />one of those "top 10 books you've probably never read written by a woman"<br /><br />as post-punk porn, post-punk feminist. <br /><br />...kathy acker's fuckin crazy. <br /><br /> what the hell did I just read <br /><br />...it’s fucked-up.<br /><br />A somewhat pointless and annoying experimental novel with silly dirty pictures drawn in it. <br /><br />...the Kathy Acker who seems to go off a prescribed psychiatric medication; in turn, a deluge of profanity, personal insults, and pornography erupts. <br /><br /> How and why was this huge waste of my time even published ?<br /><br />...written by an angry 14 year-old. <br /><br />Please, someone explain to me the literary value of this book. <br /><br />...what appears an incestuous relationship with her father. She has an abortion...<br /><br />I actually like weird, playful, vulgar books. <br /><br />...(including lots of sexual organs).<br /><br />If I owned the copy I read, I would have defaced it and thrown it away. <br /><br />...(post-punk is much milder). <br /><br />However I mostly found it embarrassing to read in public because of the smutty line drawings, offensive because of the continual use of foul language or confusing because sentence flow skipped lines of text.<br /><br />...would probably never be able to fully understand its contents… [final ellipses in original]<br /><br />If someone has a logical point to make it does not necessarily have to be laden with a metaphysical analogy to be compelling. Capitalism is not like a man raping his daughter.<br /><br />...is unreadable and makes you look forward to reading Adjunct: An Undigest.<br /><br />If you're of the opinion that punk is all rage and no talent, then yes, this is punk lit.<br /><br />...her insatiable desire for violent sex kind of contradicts those cries. <br /><br /><br /><b>before I die</b><br /><br /><br />...having your head flushed down a recently utilized commode.<br /><br />Don't waste yr time w/ this. Read the people that she rips off instead - esp Jean Genet.<br /><br />...then told their emotions and feelings are high art and should be displayed at full volume. <br /><br />"1001 books to read before you die"<br /><br />...is disgusting, poorly written, and not worth reading. <br /><br />I love a good anti-novel. I'm all up for some experimentalist literature. But what is this?<br /><br />....not to be taken lightly, all skill and power and raw emotion, this ain't it.<br /><br /> If I could ever support the horror that is <b>book burning</b>, it could done with this book.<br /><br />Over all I needed to know what happened Janey. My boyfriend looked over my shoulder quite a few times while I was reading and thought the pictures were "interesting."<br /><br />A truly awful book - 1 part The Atrocity Exhibition, ..., 3 parts crystal meth, and 7 parts blotter acid.<br /><br />There's no denying Acker's intelligence, but I found this to be a mess. Perhaps it seemed more subversive when originally published, if so, it hasn't aged well.<br /><br />Had to read this in grad school.<br />I'm usually not a fan of <b>book burning</b>.<br />But, <b>burn</b> the &amp;^%$ign book as soon as you can!<br /><br /> I could not stand this book when I first found it. Ran across it recently and realized my first instincts were just fine for me. <br /><br /> I have no idea why this has a cult following. <br /><br /> This book sucked. Big giant monkey balls. As sucky as this review is, this book sucked even worse. <br /><br /> By far the strangest, most disturbing book I've ever read. <br /><br /> I have no idea what I just read. Nothing about this book made sense at all and I do not recommend it. <br /><br /> I thought "wtf?" and "well, that was over-rated"<br /><br /> i couldn't do it. I do not normally abandon a story that I start but I could not read this book. It was miserable. <br /><br /> It's a shocker. Not shocking no, just fucking terrible. <br /><br /> I feel dirty. And confused. <br /><br />Rubbish<br /><br /> Bored me rigid.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4929529395099251503" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_39813986" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/39813986" />
    <a title="Stephanie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1702211-stephanie"><img alt="Stephanie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1583090952p2/1702211.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">people who like to read books solely for shock value</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1702211-stephanie?shelf=1001-books-read">1001-books-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1702211-stephanie?shelf=read-in-2009">read-in-2009</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1702211-stephanie?shelf=just-say-no">just-say-no</a>,

          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer39813986" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6864689283866981017">Absolutely the worst book I have ever read, and I've read a lot of books. This was horrid. The only reason I read it was because it's on the 1001 Books You Must Read Before You Die list, and I am now obsessed with reading every book on the list. Up to this point, it hadn't really steered me wrong. That streak has ended. Honestly, I have no problem with vulgar books, and I don't shy away from books with pornographic material. It wasn't the fact that the main character in this novel was sleeping w</span>
  <span id="freeText6864689283866981017" style="display:none">Absolutely the worst book I have ever read, and I've read a lot of books. This was horrid. The only reason I read it was because it's on the 1001 Books You Must Read Before You Die list, and I am now obsessed with reading every book on the list. Up to this point, it hadn't really steered me wrong. That streak has ended. Honestly, I have no problem with vulgar books, and I don't shy away from books with pornographic material. It wasn't the fact that the main character in this novel was sleeping with her father. It wasn't that she was sold into white slavery and locked in a room for months while a Peruvian man taught her to be a whore. It wasn't any of these things, although admittedly they don't make for a great story line. It was that the entire time I was reading the book I wanted to gouge my eyes out. I could have forgiven the jumping around from topic to topic and random segues - part of the story is supposed to be a journal that Janey, the main character, is keeping and part of the story is ... I don't even know what. But I just really despised the author's style. To be honest, I didn't understand how a lot of it fit together, and that's because I really didn't care to. I don't abandon books; once I've started a story I have to see it through to the bitter end. I should have just thrown this one away. Literally. I HATE giving poor reviews and can usually find at least one thing I like about a novel, but as far as I can see, this has absolutely no literary value. I just don't see how anyone could make an argument for its inclusion on a list of books you must read before you die.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6864689283866981017" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36977686-rebecca-mcnutt?shelf=women-s">women-s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36977686-rebecca-mcnutt?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1940058786" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13989280108066926387">I always have trouble with stories like these. Unlike arthouse <i>films</i> which often work on using odd concepts to create an allegorical or metaphorical story, oftentimes it doesn't come out right with books, and this is one such case.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1933013253" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1933013253" />
    <a title="Zadignose" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4319285-zadignose"><img alt="Zadignose" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1340768521p2/4319285.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1933013253?book_show_action=true">Sep 26, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Zadignose" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Zadignose" href="/user/show/4319285-zadignose">Zadignose</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4319285-zadignose?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1933013253" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11887685910946024712">This is an amazing book, hyperbolic, mad, outrageous, cynical, expressive, and audacious. Acker has portrayed an odd and often grotesque intersection of nightmare and fantasy in which the worst nightmare often is the realization of fantasy. At the same time she gives very real insight into the mind of a person whose character and vision have been shaped by a life of abuse and exploitation. She doesn't mind being crude and inelegant, and she happily presents a disjointed and often surreal narrati</span>
  <span id="freeText11887685910946024712" style="display:none">This is an amazing book, hyperbolic, mad, outrageous, cynical, expressive, and audacious. Acker has portrayed an odd and often grotesque intersection of nightmare and fantasy in which the worst nightmare often is the realization of fantasy. At the same time she gives very real insight into the mind of a person whose character and vision have been shaped by a life of abuse and exploitation. She doesn't mind being crude and inelegant, and she happily presents a disjointed and often surreal narrative... she has basically no inhibition whatsoever:<br /><br /><blockquote>"Right now I can speak as directly as I want 'cause no one gives a shit about writing and ideas, all anyone cares about is money."</blockquote><br /><br />If I could find a thesis in this book (I swear I found it at some point, but may have misplaced it along the way) it would be something about the necessity of vision and the horror of a life without dreams. Even if the visions are of being sexually abused by a filthy, sado-masochistic President Jimmy Carter with a shitty ass (one of the most disgusting characters I've ever encountered in literature), they apparently have more vitality than an ordinary job in a bakery. Robotic slaves to capitalist materialism have no escape when their dreams die. Of course, Janey the dreamer also has no escape, and her body and sanity are bound to die. She's also never really given many choices. Yet there's a romantic desperation in her vision-life.<br /><br /><blockquote>"Everything takes place at night,<br />In the centres of nightmares and dreams,<br />I know I'm being torn apart by my needs,<br />I don't know how to see anymore."</blockquote><br /><br />We need more literary terrorists like this. Kathy Acker was an innovator. At the same time, I think her voice sometimes echoes in the writings of some of today's alternative/cynical writer-outsiders. Anyway, she's left her mark.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11887685910946024712" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2093345883" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2093345883" />
    <a title="Roman Clodia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/56111743-roman-clodia"><img alt="Roman Clodia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1464021031p2/56111743.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2093345883?book_show_action=true">Aug 15, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Roman Clodia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Roman Clodia" href="/user/show/56111743-roman-clodia">Roman Clodia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/35848801-blood-and-guts-in-high-school">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2093345883" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8732585710635216967">Originally written in the 1970s, this Penguin re-release coincides with Acker's untimely death from cancer 20 years ago this year. Ever the <i>enfant terrible</i>, this is deliberately designed to provoke and shock - and that it still can in 2017 would, I think, have amused Acker vastly.<br/><br/>She throws everything into this dislocated narrative: sex, violence, disease, exploitation, corruption, abuse, incest, feminism, capitalism, anger, submission, identity, the grotesque body... with nods along the way to </span>
  <span id="freeText8732585710635216967" style="display:none">Originally written in the 1970s, this Penguin re-release coincides with Acker's untimely death from cancer 20 years ago this year. Ever the <i>enfant terrible</i>, this is deliberately designed to provoke and shock - and that it still can in 2017 would, I think, have amused Acker vastly.<br /><br />She throws everything into this dislocated narrative: sex, violence, disease, exploitation, corruption, abuse, incest, feminism, capitalism, anger, submission, identity, the grotesque body... with nods along the way to all those master-narratives that construct culture, from Freud and Marx to Hamlet and Sartre. At times deliberately disgusting, at others blackly funny (Janey's analysis of The Scarlet Letter is twisted genius!), this tears up the rule books of what 'fiction', 'narrative', a 'novel' are 'supposed' to be, and creates what only barely passes for a story via a collage of prose, dialogue, verse, drawings, scrawled capitals, 'Persian' writing. <br /><br />For all of the ways that this intersects with critical theory, the theorisation of women's writing (think Cixous' écriture féminine, Irigaray, Kristeva) and Lacanian psychoanalysis, it's also dirty and grubby and revelling in its own gleeful rebelliousness and subversive energy. Certainly not for everyone...<br /><br />Thanks to Penguin for an ARC via NetGalley.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8732585710635216967" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer15328468028096826462">Here's a polarizing one, huh? I read some Acker in college - you know, back when my hair was purple, my stereo was screaming with angry grrrl singers, and the world was just. not. ready. for. my. brilliance! I liked it then. It felt subversive and important. In fact, it felt a whole lot like all the shit I woke up at 5pm and read that I wrote on a youthful rebellious cocaine binge when I was about 19 years old.   <span id="freeText15328468028096826462" style="display:none">Here's a polarizing one, huh? I read some Acker in college - you know, back when my hair was purple, my stereo was screaming with angry grrrl singers, and the world was just. not. ready. for. my. brilliance! I liked it then. It felt subversive and important. In fact, it felt a whole lot like all the shit I woke up at 5pm and read that I wrote on a youthful rebellious cocaine binge when I was about 19 years old. Bad words, sex, cunt, piss, shit, run on sentences, angst angst angst. <br /><br />Yeah. I'm 34 now. And I'm just not feeling it anymore.<br /><br />I get the deconstruction, the post-structuralism, the neo-sex-positive-feminism argument. <div id="review_58418420" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/58418420" />
    <a title="Jamie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1118970-jamie"><img alt="Jamie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1512217497p2/1118970.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/58418420?book_show_action=true">Jun 04, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jamie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jamie" href="/user/show/1118970-jamie">Jamie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1118970-jamie?shelf=1001-books">1001-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1118970-jamie?shelf=read-in-2012">read-in-2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1118970-jamie?shelf=field-exam-reading-list">field-exam-reading-list</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer58418420" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10158618791304413300">One of the most fucked up books I've ever read. I can see, certainly, how it would annoy some in a stylistic manner and infuriate others on a conceptual level (for example, Janey is being...raped?...by her father, but Acker presents this relationship in the terms of a dying love affair). Let's say it's not for the weak of heart, nor is it recommended to those without a sense of incredibly, powerfully grotesque and debasing humor. <br/><br/>Thankfully, I'm a kind of monster with no boundaries! So this was </span>
  <span id="freeText10158618791304413300" style="display:none">One of the most fucked up books I've ever read. I can see, certainly, how it would annoy some in a stylistic manner and infuriate others on a conceptual level (for example, Janey is being...raped?...by her father, but Acker presents this relationship in the terms of a dying love affair). Let's say it's not for the weak of heart, nor is it recommended to those without a sense of incredibly, powerfully grotesque and debasing humor. <br /><br />Thankfully, I'm a kind of monster with no boundaries! So this was an absolute joyride to me. By the time Janey is describing Jimmy Carter's asshole caked in several inches of old shit, and referring to him as a syphilitic pustule that fell off of Nixon's dick, I was nearly crying with laughter in the middle of the Boston Public Garden - likely looking like a total maniac, to boot. Jean Genet makes a wonderfully bizarre appearance, and the "letter" from Erica Jong was likewise too fucking funny to get offended by. If there's ever been a line of appropriateness drawn in any context, Acker's novel pukes on it and keeps on trucking. <br /><br />Like I said, not for everyone, but if you're totally fucked in the head or the funny bone, you should definitely give it a test run. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10158618791304413300" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText8814769405495531234" style="display:none">We've been punked, folks.   <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/380074221" />
    <a title="Ellinor" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8307014-ellinor"><img alt="Ellinor" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1573658130p2/8307014.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ellinor" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ellinor" href="/user/show/8307014-ellinor">Ellinor</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/35848801-blood-and-guts-in-high-school">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8307014-ellinor?shelf=1001-books-2006">1001-books-2006</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8307014-ellinor?shelf=1001-books">1001-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8307014-ellinor?shelf=1001-books-core-list">1001-books-core-list</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8307014-ellinor?shelf=netgalley">netgalley</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer380074221" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4112251410762938972">So this is story about Janey, a ten-yea-old girl, half-orphan, living with her father who to her is "boyfriend, brother, sister, money, amusement, and father" - and we might add sexual partner. First he rapes her, then she willingly has sex with him because it makes her feel loved. She is suffering from pelvic inflammatory disease, has her first abortion with 13, her second one a month later. Her father sends her to New York City, where apparrently she lives on her own, joins a gang and later is</span>
  <span id="freeText4112251410762938972" style="display:none">So this is story about Janey, a ten-yea-old girl, half-orphan, living with her father who to her is "boyfriend, brother, sister, money, amusement, and father" - and we might add sexual partner. First he rapes her, then she willingly has sex with him because it makes her feel loved. She is suffering from pelvic inflammatory disease, has her first abortion with 13, her second one a month later. Her father sends her to New York City, where apparrently she lives on her own, joins a gang and later is kidnapped, held captive and taught how to be a prostitute. At the age of 14 she gets cancer and dies.<br /><br />Blood and Guts in High School clearly is a book not for everyone - including me. It is just the type of book I as a teenager would have called "problem book" and which I already hated back then. It now is a Penguin Modern Classic and I really don't see the reason why because usually the titles in this series are of a certain quality. The only reason I'm rating this book two stars instead of one is the writing technique Kathy Acker uses - collage. The story is told from Janey's perspective but alternates between first and third person narration. There are multiple drawings (especially ones of sexual organs), poems, letters etc. (for example the first scene of the book is written as a drama scene). This could have made a decent book but I had a problem with Janey's thoughts. At the end of the book she is 14 but hardly had any education. During her imprisonment she reads The Scarlet Letter, completely understands it and knows how to interpret it. This is quite unlikely as are many of her other thoughts - they are the thoughts of someone maybe 20 years old or older.<br /><br />(I received a free digital copy via Netgalley/ the publisher. Thanks for the opportunity!)</span>
  <a data-text-id="4112251410762938972" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText17345329583992445243" style="display:none">I ran into Acker via these kids I lived next door to when I was about 23. They ate a lot of E and Acid. I was listening a lot of Patti Smith and PJ Harvey at the time and they were like "here, Kathy Acker" and I read it and I really liked it. You can analyze Acker's work all you want. I'm sure it's important/powerful/whatever but if you aren't between the ages of lets say 16 and 23 you might miss out on how much brutal fun this book is.<br /><br />At current, it doesn't rank as something I'd be that interested in (though at the time it blew my mind) but her style, her ruthlessness, the raw sexuality, the insanity and craziness of her books (this one in particular) her daring to use and abuse language really liberated my creative process. </span>
