    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_34417582" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/34417582" />
    <a title="Melanie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/245892-melanie"><img alt="Melanie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1520462334p2/245892.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/34417582?book_show_action=true">Oct 02, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melanie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melanie" href="/user/show/245892-melanie">Melanie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/245892-melanie?shelf=2009">2009</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer34417582" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15257330581418119884">Sometimes I wonder if there's something wrong with my brain, or at least with my memory/reading comprehension/retention abilities. This happens when I read multiple works by the same author in a short period of time; I go on a spree, devouring everything I can find by a particular writer, and at some point, I'll find myself unable to tell one book from another. It doesn't seem to be related to the quality of the author's work--I've experienced this sensation with feline-centric mystery novels pe</span>
  <span id="freeText15257330581418119884" style="display:none">Sometimes I wonder if there's something wrong with my brain, or at least with my memory/reading comprehension/retention abilities. This happens when I read multiple works by the same author in a short period of time; I go on a spree, devouring everything I can find by a particular writer, and at some point, I'll find myself unable to tell one book from another. It doesn't seem to be related to the quality of the author's work--I've experienced this sensation with feline-centric mystery novels penned by elderly women, and with Updike's WASPy fables, and even with the masterworks of Saint Nabokov. I'm beginning to think that it's not them, it's me. <br /><br />So I'm hitting that point with Kathy Acker now, and I don't know who's to blame for my overwhelming feeling of hey-that-tree-looks-familiar. Have I gotten The Gist of her work now? Am I just sticking around for a few cool phrases here and there, but no longer able to differentiate between characters, narratives, etc.? Should I have taken a break between readings? Should I have read her works chronologically? <i>Did I do something wrong?</i><br /><br />I don't know. The first book of hers that I read (<i>Don Quixote</i>) was perfect for the mood I was in; the second (<i>In Memoriam to Identity</i>) makes use of a text (<i>The Sound and the Fury</i>) that's practically tattooed on my heart. It was dumb luck that I started with those two, and I can see how my interest in Acker could have been altered if I <i>hadn't</i> stumbled upon the most specific-to-me books at that time, in that order, under those circumstances. <br /><br />It's hard to write a review of <i>Blood and Guts in High School</i> because a.) it's all mixed up in my mind with Acker's other stuff, and b.) I'm so far into Ackerland right now that I can't be objective about it. <br /><br />I mean, the writing is sharp, of course. Every page seems to have something that's brutal, some knife-twist of words or emotion or intellect. The concerns that I recognize as Acker's are also there--sex, politics, identity, illness--as are the engagements with literature and philosophy. And, hell, she made me <i>almost</i> consider re-reading <i>The Scarlet Letter</i>, so that's impressive. <br /><br />But this book does test my limits as a reader. It's like...Burroughs. In high school, I loved William S. Burroughs, and I did the same thing with him then that I'm doing with Kathy Acker now, just slashing and burning through everything I can get my hands on. But I found that some of his more self-consciously experimental stuff began to try my patience; the cut-up method quickly lost its novelty and my interest started to wane. And now, with <i>Blood and Guts...</i>, the illustrations and poetic fragments just...began to wear on me, a bit. This probably makes me a bad postmodernist, but I'll admit it anyway! <br /><br />So all of this is to say that I think I would have written a different review of this book had I read it before falling for the Acker Mystique. Of course, in the same way that Wile E. Coyote can only run through the air when he doesn't realize he's darted off a cliff, if I start thinking stuff like "There's no way to be objective when reviewing a book (or anything)," GoodReads may very well cease to exist. Hmm. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15257330581418119884" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2546233046" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2546233046" />
    <a title="Lee Foust" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13984676-lee-foust"><img alt="Lee Foust" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442875445p2/13984676.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2546233046?book_show_action=true">Oct 08, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lee Foust" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lee Foust" href="/user/show/13984676-lee-foust">Lee Foust</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780394623344" href="/book/show/3070407-blood-and-guts-in-high-school">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13984676-lee-foust?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2546233046" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1764607686799771908">I think each Kathy Acker novel I re-read I decide is her best and say so in my review. Kathy would enjoy, I think, these contradictions. This one is super juvenile and also the most free-feeling of all of her novels--there are silly naughty drawings of genitalia, maps of dreams in the form of a pirate map sorta, scripts, a Persion lesson notebook in the form of a poem, just plain old regular prose, a book report on <i>The Scarlet Letter</i> that totally makes me want to read it again... a little bit of</span>
  <span id="freeText1764607686799771908" style="display:none">I think each Kathy Acker novel I re-read I decide is her best and say so in my review. Kathy would enjoy, I think, these contradictions. This one is super juvenile and also the most free-feeling of all of her novels--there are silly naughty drawings of genitalia, maps of dreams in the form of a pirate map sorta, scripts, a Persion lesson notebook in the form of a poem, just plain old regular prose, a book report on <i>The Scarlet Letter</i> that totally makes me want to read it again... a little bit of everything--and it's all so fucking great. The world so needs this dirty, wacky, brilliant little book about the fuckery and glory of being 18, horny, translating Sextus Propertius in Latin class, reading Nathaniel Hawthorn, hanging out with Jean Genet in your dreams, and feeling really fucked up and ambivalent about the growing itch between your legs. Thank God for <i>Blood and Guts in High School</i>--the only young adult novel that tells it like it really is.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1764607686799771908" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('6F4c8lHFE6Yl66EmCUggJlpahDqQmb9AK0vzPR2rZct33It0l0LOPJKaHnRpS8L2VKQEcEozrkQfaXMSoHB5Zg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('LbsIeIK0Ld72YpaIUVsfxeDbV9tYaidvYs3e8FLn7KWyOZ/+RDPwREETKdoxWP0V7iXXkYLANmtW717f7zzwCA==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

In his attempt to kill his father, Berg mutilates a ventriloquist\'s dummy, almost falls victim to his father\'s mistaken sexual advances, and is relentlessly taunted by a group of tramps. Nelson was born a few years after Jane’s death, and the narrative is suffused with the long shadow her murder cast over both the family and her psyche.Jane explores the nature of this haunting incident via a collage of poetry, prose, and documentary sources, including newspapers, related &quot;true crime&quot; books, and fragments from Jane’s own diaries written.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/6304.Kathy_Acker">About Kathy Acker</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/6304.Kathy_Acker"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1208972279p3/6304.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer15812832719532072151">Born of German-Jewish stock, Kathy Acker was brought up by her mother and stepfather (her natural father left her mother before Kathy was born) in a prosperous district of NY. At 18, she left home and worked as a stripper. Her involvement in the sex industry helped to make her a hit on the NY art scene, and she was photographed by the newly fashionable Robert Mapplethorpe. Preferring to be known s</span>
  <span id="freeText15812832719532072151" style="display:none">Born of German-Jewish stock, Kathy Acker was brought up by her mother and stepfather (her natural father left her mother before Kathy was born) in a prosperous district of NY. At 18, she left home and worked as a stripper. Her involvement in the sex industry helped to make her a hit on the NY art scene, and she was photographed by the newly fashionable Robert Mapplethorpe. Preferring to be known simply as 'Acker' (the name she took from her first husband Robert, and which she continued to use even after a short-lived second marriage to composer Peter Gordon), she moved to London in the mid-eighties and stayed in Britain for five years.<br /><br />Acker's writing is as difficult to classify into any particular genre as she herself was. She writes fluidly, operating in the borderlands and junkyards of human experience. Her work is experimental, playful, and provocative, engagingly alienating, narratively non sequitur. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15812832719532072151" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
